Li'an Invalid When Wife or Husband Lacks Capacity
Ruling that li'an requires capacity for hadd and testimony

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios where li'an is invalid due to lack of capacity and explain the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute regarding the mute husband.

Core Idea
Li'an requires both spouses to have full legal capacity and the accusation to be explicit; otherwise it is invalid.

Li'an (mutual cursing) is a severe procedure that stands in place of the hadd penalty for false accusation of zina. Its validity depends on strict conditions, including the legal capacity of both spouses and the explicit nature of the accusation. This passage from al-Hidayah examines three cases where li'an is invalid: when the wife is a minor or insane, when the husband is a minor or insane, and when the husband is mute. The Hanafi school relies on the principle that hudud (penalties) are averted by ambiguities (shubuhat), leading to a disagreement with Imam al-Shafi'i on the mute's case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios of invalidity
The passage lists three cases: (1) wife is minor or insane, (2) husband is minor or insane, (3) husband is mute. Note the common thread: each involves a lack of full legal capacity or an ambiguous accusation.
All three prevent li'an from being valid.
2
Understand the analogy with hadd qadhf
Li'an is the substitute for the hadd penalty of flogging that would be imposed on a stranger who falsely accuses a woman of zina. Since a stranger would not be flogged for accusing a minor or insane woman (because they are not subject to hadd), the husband also does not perform li'an. Similarly, if the husband lacks capacity (minor/insane), he cannot testify, which is essential for li'an.
Li'an stands in place of hadd, so its preconditions mirror those of hadd.
3
Analyze the condition of explicit accusation (sarih)
Li'an requires the husband's accusation to be explicit. A mute's gesture or ambiguous words are not sarih, so li'an does not apply. The Hanafi school invokes the maxim 'hudud are averted by shubuhat' (ambiguities).
The mute's inability to produce sarih speech creates a shubhah that blocks li'an.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that a mute's gesture is equivalent to speech; thus, an explicit accusation can be established through signs, making li'an valid. The Hanafi response stresses that the gesture cannot be as explicit as speech, and the presence of shubhah must avert the hadd and its substitute.
The thamarah al-khilaf: Hanafi says no li'an; Shafi'i says li'an occurs.
5
Apply the principle of dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat
This overarching principle governs all cases: any ambiguity in the accusation prevents the application of hadd and, by extension, li'an. The principle is based on the prophetic hadith 'Idra'u al-hududa bi al-shubuhat'.
This is a key usul al-fiqh principle in Hanafi penal law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واذا قذف امرأته وهى صغيرة او مجنونة فلا لعان بينهما لانه لا يحد قاذفها لو كان اجنبيا فكذا لايلاعن الزوج لقيامه مقامه وكذا اذا كان الزوج صغيرا او مجنونا لعدم اهلية الشهادة وقذف الاخرس لا يتعلق به اللعان لانه يتعلق بالصريح كحد القذف وفيه خلاف الشافعى وهذا لانه لا يعرى عن الشبهة والحدود تندرئ بها
ترجمہ اور اگر کسی شخص نے اپنی بیوی کو زنا کا عیب لگایا حالانکہ وہ بچی ہے، یا دیوانی ہے تو ان دونوں میں لعان نہ ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اس کے تہمت لگانے والے کو حد نہیں ماری جاتی اگر چہ اجنبی ہو۔ پس ایسے ہی شوہر سے لعان نہ کرایا جائے اس لئے کہ لعان حد قذف کے قائم مقام ہے۔ اور ایسے ہی جب شوہر بچہ ہو یا دیوانہ ہو ( تو بھی لعان نہیں) کیونکہ شوہر میں شہادت کی لیاقت نہیں ہے۔ اور گونگے کا تہمت لگانا اس کے ساتھ بھی لعان متعلق نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ لعان صریح قذف سے متعلق ہوتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ حد قذف اور اس میں امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے اور یہ اس لئے کہ گونگے کا کہنا شبہ سے خالی نہیں اور حدود شبہات کی وجہ سے دور کر دی جاتی ہیں۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی کو زنا کا عیب لگایا درانحالیکہ وہ نابالغہ بچی ہے یا پاگل ہے تو ان دونوں میاں بیوی میں لعان نہیں ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ صغیرہ اور مجنونہ کے قاذف کو حد نہیں ماری جاتی ، اگر چہ ان کا قاذف اجنبی مرد ہی کیوں نہ ہو ۔ حالانکہ لعان کے شرائط میں سے یہ ہے کہ عورت ایسی ہو جس کے قاذف کو حد قذف ماری جائے ۔ پس اسی طرح شوہر سے لعان کا مطالبہ نہیں ہوگا کیونکہ لعان حد قذف کے قائم مقام ہے۔ اور یہی حکم اس وقت ہے جب کہ شو ہر نابالغ بچہ ہو یا پاگل ہو کیونکہ یہ دونوں شریعت کے مخاطب نہ ہونے کی وجہ سے شہادت کی لیاقت نہیں رکھتے ہیں۔ حالانکہ لعان کے واسطے ضروری ہے کہ زوجین شہادت کی اہلیت رکھتے ہوں۔ اور اگر گونگے نے اپنی بیوی کو زنا کا عیب لگایا تو اس سے بھی لعان متعلق نہیں ہوگا۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ لعان صریح قذف سے متعلق ہوتا ہے جیسا کہ حد قذف صریح قذف سے واجب ہوتی ہے۔ اس میں امام شافعی کا اختلاف ہے وہ فرماتے ہیں کہ گونگے کا اشارہ ایسا ہے جیسا کہ بولنے والے کا کلام کرنا اور گونگے کے اشارے سے لعان کا واجب نہ ہونا اس لئے ہے کہ گونگے کے زنا کا عیب لگانے میں شبہ موجود ہے اور شبہات کی وجہ سے حدود دور کر دی جاتی ہیں۔
And if a man accuses his wife of zina while she is a minor or insane, there is no li'an between them because if he were a stranger, her accuser would not be flogged. Similarly, the husband does not perform li'an because he stands in his place. And likewise if the husband is a minor or insane due to lack of capacity for testimony. And the accusation of a mute is not related to li'an because it is related to explicit (sarih) like the hadd of qadhf. There is a disagreement of al-Shafi'i on this, because the mute's statement is not free from ambiguity, and hudud are averted by ambiguities.
Source pages p. 153

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding conditions for li'an (mutual cursing). It addresses three scenarios where li'an is invalid due to lack of capacity. First, if the wife is a minor or insane, li'an does not occur because the husband would not be flogged for qadhf (false accusation) if he were a stranger, since such women are not subject to hadd. Li'an stands in place of hadd, so it also falls. Second, if the husband is a minor or insane, he lacks legal capacity (ahliyyah) to testify, which is necessary for li'an. Third, if the husband is mute, his accusation is not explicit (sarih) but involves ambiguity (shubhah), and hudud (penalties) are averted by ambiguities. Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees on the mute, holding that his gesture is equivalent to speech. The Hanafi response relies on the principle of 'dar' al-hudud bi al-shubuhat' (hudud are averted by ambiguities). The commentator explains each point in detail, emphasizing the analogy with hadd and the necessity of explicit speech.

Key Terms
صغيرة (saghirah): A minor female who has not reached puberty
مجنونة (majnunah): An insane woman lacking legal capacity
أخرس (akhras): A mute person who cannot speak
شبهة (shubhah): Ambiguity or doubt that averts hudud

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is li'an invalid if the wife is a minor?
A) Because she cannot testify
B) Because a stranger would not be flogged for accusing her of zina
C) Because she is not of age
D) Because the husband is not required to pay hadd

Key Takeaway
Li'an is invalid when either spouse lacks capacity (minor/insane) or the accusation is not explicit (mute); hudud are averted by shubuhat.

The Conditional Denial of Paternity and Li'an
Intra-Hanafi dispute on li'an from a paternity denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi school rules that a conditional denial of pregnancy does not trigger li'an and apply the mufta bihi position to a new scenario.

Core Idea
A conditional denial of paternity does not trigger li'an because qadhf cannot be conditional.

A husband says to his wife: 'Your pregnancy is not from me.' Is this a direct accusation of zina requiring li'an (mutual cursing) or merely a conditional statement? The Hanafi school is internally divided on this issue. In this lesson, you will dissect the arguments of Imam Abu Hanifa (supported by Zufar) and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani), understand the logical underpinnings of each, and learn which position is the operative ruling (mufta bihi) in the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Statement
Locate the exact phrase in the source: 'laysa hamluki minni' (your pregnancy is not from me). Understand that this is not an explicit accusation of zina but a denial of paternity.
The statement does not use the word zina or 'whore'.
2
State the Position of Abu Hanifa & Zufar
Abu Hanifa and Zufar hold that this statement does not trigger li'an because at the moment of utterance, there is no certainty (yaqin) that the wife is actually pregnant. The husband may be mistaken (e.g., bloating mistaken for pregnancy). Hence he is not a qadhif (accuser).
Certainty is key for qadhf.
3
State the Position of the Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that if the wife gives birth within six months (the minimum pregnancy duration), then it becomes certain that she was pregnant at the time of the denial. Therefore, the statement becomes a retroactive qadhf and li'an is wajib.
Six months is the minimum gestation in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyse the Hanafi Response to Sahibayn
The commentator (Hanafi tradition) counters that the denial is effectively conditional: 'If you are pregnant, it is not from me.' Qadhf cannot be conditional (ta'liq bi'l-shart). Since the statement is conditional, it cannot be valid qadhf. Hence, li'an is not triggered.
The principle: qadhf must be explicit and unconditional.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa: no li'an from such a statement. This is also the view of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
Refer to the Urdu commentary's indication.

And if the husband says 'Your pregnancy is not from me,' there is no li'an. This is the statement of Abu Hanifa and Zufar, because he is not certain of the existence of the pregnancy, so he does not become an accuser. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: Li'an becomes obligatory by denying the pregnancy if she brings a child in less than six months, and this is the meaning of what is mentioned in al-Asl, because we are certain of the existence of the pregnancy at that time, so qadhf is realized. We say: When it is not qadhf at that moment, it becomes like a conditional statement, as if he said: 'If you are pregnant, it is not from me.' Qadhf is not valid when conditioned.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa (with Zufar) and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding the ruling when a husband says to his wife 'laysa hamluki minni' (your pregnancy is not from me). According to Abu Hanifa and Zufar, this statement does not trigger li'an because at the time of utterance, there is no certainty that the wife is actually pregnant. She might have a bloated stomach mistaken for pregnancy. Hence, the husband is not considered a qadhif (accuser of zina), and li'an is not wajib. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, if the wife gives birth within less than six months from the time of the statement, it becomes certain that she was pregnant at the time, so qadhf is established and li'an becomes wajib. The commentator, following the Hanafi tradition, supports Abu Hanifa's view on the grounds that the statement is effectively conditional ('if you are pregnant, then it's not from me'), and qadhf cannot be conditional. The principle is that qadhf must be explicit and unconditional. Hence, the mufta bihi position is that li'an is not wajib from such a statement.

Key Terms
ليس حملک منى (laysa hamluki minni): The statement 'your pregnancy is not from me'
تعليق بالشرط (ta'liq bi'l-shart): Conditioning a legal effect on a future event
أقل الحمل (aqall al-haml): Minimum duration of pregnancy: six months

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) No, never
D) Yes, if the wife is pregnant

Key Takeaway
A conditional denial of paternity does not trigger li'an in the Hanafi school because qadhf must be explicit and unconditional.

Explicit Zina Accusation and Li'an
Ruling that explicit zina accusation triggers li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an explicit qadhf (sarih) and state that it makes li'an wajib, distinguishing it from ambiguous denial of paternity.

Core Idea
Explicit zina accusation triggers li'an; mere denial of paternity does not.

In family law, a husband may deny paternity or accuse his wife of adultery. This passage clarifies that only an explicit accusation of zina (sarih qadhf) obligates li'an. An ambiguous denial like 'this is not my child' does not suffice. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying the rules of li'an.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's statement
Read the husband's words carefully. Does he explicitly mention zina or just deny paternity? Here he says 'zanaitu' (you committed adultery) and 'hadha al-hamlu min al-zina' (this pregnancy is from adultery).
Explicit mention of zina is key.
2
Classify as sarih qadhf
The Hanafi school defines sarih (explicit) as leaving no room for interpretation. This statement clearly accuses the wife of zina, so it is sarih qadhf.
Sarih qadhf triggers hadd if not proven by the accuser, and here it triggers li'an because the accuser is the spouse.
3
Apply the ruling
Because the accusation is sarih and the spouses are married, li'an becomes wajib (obligatory). The passage says 'tala'ana' (they perform li'an).
Li'an is the mutual oath-taking that severs the marriage.
4
Contrast with ambiguous denial
If the husband only said 'laysa hamluki minni' (this pregnancy is not from me), it is not sarih qadhf because zina is not mentioned. Li'an would not be wajib based solely on that.
The key difference: mention of zina vs. mere denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فان قال لهاز نيت وهذا الحبل من الزناء تلاعنا لوجود القذف حيث ذكر الزناء صريحا ولم ينف القاضي
اگر كهاز نيت وهذا الحبل من الزناء دونوں لعان کریں
If he says 'You committed adultery and this pregnancy is from adultery,' they perform li'an because of the existence of qadhf, as he mentioned zina explicitly and did not deny the pregnancy.
Source pages p. 154

Commentary
This passage is a brief ruling: when the husband explicitly accuses his wife of zina and states that the pregnancy is from that zina, li'an becomes wajib. The reason is that there is a clear, explicit (sarih) charge of zina, and he does not merely deny paternity. Unlike the previous case where he only said 'laysa hamluki minni' (which is ambiguous), here he explicitly mentions zina, so the qadhf is unambiguous. The Urdu commentary confirms that both spouses perform li'an (tala'ana). Note that the passage also says 'lam yanfi al-qadi'? Actually 'wa lam yanfi al-qadi' might be a misread; it probably means 'and he did not deny the pregnancy' (lam yanfi al-haml) but the text says 'al-qadi'? The Urdu says 'دونوں لعان کریں' meaning 'both perform li'an.'

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit statement leaving no room for ambiguity
قذف (qadhf): False accusation of zina

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the husband says 'the pregnancy is not from me'
B) When the husband explicitly says 'you committed zina and this pregnancy is from zina'
C) When the husband is mute and gestures
D) When the wife is a minor

Key Takeaway
An explicit accusation of zina (sarih qadhf) by a husband against his wife makes li'an wajib; an ambiguous denial does not.

Denial of Paternity During Pregnancy in Li'an
Hanafi ruling that paternity cannot be denied during pregnancy, with Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that paternity cannot be denied during pregnancy and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith of Hilal.

Core Idea
Rulings on a child attach only after birth; denial during pregnancy is invalid without certain knowledge.

The timing of denying paternity in li'an is a point of contention between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools. While Imam al-Shafi'i relies on the precedent of the Prophet (peace be upon him) denying the child from Hilal during pregnancy, the Hanafi school insists that such a ruling is specific to revealed knowledge. In this lesson, we will unpack the reasoning behind the Hanafi position and understand the practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: The husband cannot deny paternity of a child during pregnancy; the judge does not accept such a denial.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds the opposing view: he allows denial of paternity during pregnancy.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i dalil: the hadith of Hilal where the Prophet denied the child while the wife was pregnant.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the Prophet's action was based on revelation, not a general rule; the child's features were described, confirming certain knowledge.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: under Hanafi law, the child remains attributed to the husband until after birth; under Shafi'i, the judge may sever lineage before birth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحمل وقال الشافعي ينفيه لانه عليه السلام نفى الولد عن هلال وقد قذفها حاملا ولنا ان الاحكام لاتترتب عليه الابعد الولادة لتمكن الاحتمال قبله والحديث محمول على انه عرف قيام الحبل بطريق الوحي ترجمہ اور اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا تو نے زنا کیا ہے اور یہ عمل زنا سے ہے، تو دونوں لعان کریں قذف کے پائے جانے کی وجہ سے۔ کیونکہ اس نے زنا کا لفظ صراحتا ذ کر کیا ہے اور قاضی حمل کو (اس مرد سے ) نفی نہ کرے گا اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ حمل کی نفی کرے۔ کیونکہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے بلال سے بچہ کی نفی کی۔ حالانکہ ہلال نے اس عورت کو حاملہ ہونے کی حالت میں عیب لگایا تھا اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ حمل پر حکم مرتب نہیں ہوتا مگر پیدا ہونے کے بعد کیونکہ پیدا ہونے سے پہلے احتمال موجود ہے۔ (کہ شاید حمل نہ ہو) اور حدیث اس بات پر محمول ہے کہ آنحضرت صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے عمل موجود ہو نا وحی کے ذریعہ معلوم کر لیا تھا۔ تشریح مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر شوہر نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا کہ تو نے زنا کیا ہے اور یہ عمل زنا سے ہے تو اس صورت میں میاں بیوی دونوں لعان کریں گے دلیل یہ ہے کہ صراحتا لفظ زنان کور ہونے کی وجہ سے زنا کا عیب لگانا پایا گیا۔ البتہ قاضی اس حمل کے نسب کی نفی نہیں کرے گا اور امام شافعی نے فرمایا کہ قاضی حمل کے نسب کی نفی کر دے۔ امام شافعی کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ حضور نے ہلال سے عورت کے بچہ کی نفی کی تھی ، حالانکہ بلال نے اس عورت کو حاملہ ہونے کی حالت میں عیب لگا یا تھا۔ اور ہماری دلیل یہ ہے کہ بچہ کی نفی کرنا بچہ کے احکام میں سے ایک حکم ہے اور احکام ولد مرتب ہوتے ہیں ولادت کے بعد نہ کہ والا تے سے پہلے اس لئے کہ ولادت سے پہلے شبہ موجود ہے۔ یعنی ہو سکتا ہے کہ حمل نہ ہو بلکہ مرض سے خون جم گیا ہو۔ اور حدیث ہلال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حدیث اس بات پر محمول ہے کہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے حمل موجود وحی کے ذریہ سے قوم کر لیا تھا اور قرینہ اس پر یہ ہے کہ حضور صلی اللہ علیہ وسلم نے فرمایا کہ اگر بچہ ایسی اور ایسی شکل کا ہو تو بلال کا ہے اور اگر ایسی اور امین شکل کا ہو تو شریک بن شما ء کا ہے اور ظاہر ہے کہ یہ بات بطریق وحی ہی کہی جاسکتی ہے۔
Regarding the pregnancy: Al-Shafi'i says he (the husband) may deny it because the Prophet (peace be upon him) denied the child from Hilal while he had accused his wife [of adultery] when she was pregnant. Our proof: legal rulings are not established from it except after birth because of the possibility [of doubt] before it; and the hadith is interpreted to mean that he (the Prophet) knew of the existence of the pregnancy through revelation. Translation: And if the husband said to his wife, 'You committed adultery and this [child] is from adultery,' then both shall perform li'an due to the occurrence of qadhf because he explicitly mentioned the word zina. The judge will not deny the pregnancy (from this man). Imam Shafi'i said he should deny the pregnancy because the Prophet denied the child from Hilal even though Hilal had accused his wife when she was pregnant. Our proof: the ruling on denial does not apply except after birth because before birth there is possibility (that it may not be a pregnancy). The hadith is interpreted to mean that the Prophet knew of the existence of the pregnancy through revelation. Explanation: The issue is: if the husband says to his wife, 'You committed adultery and this [child] is from adultery,' then both husband and wife will perform li'an because explicitly mentioning the word zina constitutes accusation of zina. However, the judge will not deny the lineage of the pregnancy. Imam Shafi'i said the judge should deny the lineage of the pregnancy. Imam Shafi'i's proof: the Prophet denied the child of Hilal from his wife, even though Hilal accused her when she was pregnant. Our proof: denial of a child is one of the rulings regarding children, and rulings regarding children are established after birth, not before, because before birth there is doubt—it is possible that it is not a pregnancy but rather blood that has congealed due to illness. The response to the hadith of Hilal: it is interpreted to mean that the Prophet knew of the existence of the pregnancy through revelation, and the evidence for that is that the Prophet said, 'If the child is of such and such form, it is from Hilal; if of such and such form, it is from Sharik ibn Sham'a.' And it is clear that this could only be said through revelation.
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi school and Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the denial of paternity during pregnancy in the context of li'an. The Hanafi position is that the husband cannot deny paternity of a child that has not yet been born. If the husband accuses his wife of adultery and says the child in her womb is from that act, both spouses perform li'an, but the judge does not deny lineage based on pregnancy alone. The reason is that legal rulings concerning a child only attach after birth; before birth, there is a possibility that the pregnancy may not result in a live child or that it is not a true pregnancy (e.g., blood clot). Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the Prophet denied paternity in the case of Hilal even though the wife was pregnant, so it is permissible. The Hanafi response is that the Prophet knew through revelation that the pregnancy was real, so his ruling was based on certain knowledge, not a general rule. The Hanafis also point to the Prophet's description of the child's features as evidence of revelatory knowledge. Thus, in normal circumstances where revelation is absent, denial cannot occur before birth. The authority weight: This is from Zahir al-Riwayah as presented in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
لعن (la'an): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.
قذف (qadhf): Accusing a married person of adultery or denying paternity, which incurs hadd punishment if not proven.
نفي الولد (nafy al-walad): Denial of paternity by the husband through li'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why can a husband not deny paternity of a child during pregnancy?
A) Because pregnancy is not certain
B) Because the Prophet never did so
C) Because the husband cannot accuse his wife of adultery while pregnant
D) Because the child must be born to have legal rulings

Key Takeaway
Paternity denial is only effective after the child is born because legal rulings on a child's lineage attach at birth.

Time Frame for Paternity Denial: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the time limit for paternity denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on the allowable period for denying paternity after birth, and apply the Hanafi mufta bihi ruling to scenarios involving indicators of acceptance.

Core Idea
Actions speak louder than days: Abu Hanifa makes denial depend on acceptance indicators, not a fixed period.

This passage addresses a critical question in family law: how long can a husband wait before denying paternity of a newborn? The Hanafi school has two positions within itself. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that there is no fixed time; instead, the husband's actions—like accepting congratulations or buying baby supplies—indicate acceptance and terminate the right to denial. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) set a fixed period of nifas (maximum 40 days) during which denial is valid regardless of actions. This dispute affects real cases, especially when the husband is absent or delays. Let us examine the proofs and the practical difference.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
Identify the ruling according to Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. Abu Hanifa: no fixed time, validity depends on indicators of acceptance. Sahibayn: validity only during nifas (40 days).
2
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
They use qiyas: immediate denial is valid, long delay invalid, so they set nifas as the boundary because it is short and tied to childbirth.
3
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
He argues that setting a fixed time is arbitrary because people differ in their need for reflection. Instead, he looks to external indicators (qarinat) that show acceptance.
4
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, if the husband accepts congratulations within nifas, denial becomes invalid. Under Sahibayn, denial remains valid until nifas ends.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and note the absentee case
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, as favored by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah. The text also handles the case of an absent husband: the period counts from his knowledge.

And if a man denies his wife's child immediately after birth, or at the time when congratulations are accepted and the tools of childbirth are purchased, his denial is valid and he performs li'an because of it. If he denies after that, he performs li'an and the lineage is established. This is according to Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: his denial is valid during the period of nifas (postpartum bleeding) because denial is valid in a short period but not in a long period. So we differentiate between them by the period of nifas because it is an effect of childbirth. His (Abu Hanifa's) proof: there is no meaning to setting a time, because time is for reflection and people's conditions differ. So we consider what indicates [acceptance]: his acceptance of congratulations, or his silence when congratulations are offered, or his purchasing of childbirth supplies, or the passage of that time while he refrains from denial. And if he was absent and did not know of the birth, then returned, the period we mentioned is considered according to the two principles (of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn). Explanation: This passage mentions several scenarios of the father's denial: (1) denying immediately after birth, (2) denying at the time when congratulations are accepted, (3) denying when supplies for birth are purchased. In these three cases, denial is valid—meaning the child's lineage is not established from the husband, and due to this denial the husband performs li'an. If he denies after these times, he performs li'an (but lineage is established). This is the madhhab of Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn said: denial within the period of nifas is valid. Their proof: denial is valid in a short period but not in a long period; the separator between a short and long period is the nifas period—i.e., nifas is a short period and beyond it is long. They made nifas the separator because it is an effect of childbirth, and in al-Mabsut it is stated that the nifas period is like the state of childbirth—during it she cannot pray or fast. Imam Abu Hanifa's proof: setting a specific time has no meaning because time is for reflection and people's conditions differ. So we consider the things that indicate non-denial: accepting congratulations, silence when congratulated, purchasing childbirth supplies, or the passage of time without denial. All these indicate that the husband is happy with the birth and considers the child his own; if he denies after this, his denial is not valid. And if the husband was absent and did not know of the birth, then returned, according to Imam Abu Hanifa the relevant time is what he indicated as a principle, and according to the Sahibayn the relevant period is as they stated.
Source pages p. 156

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the time frame within which a husband may validly deny paternity of a child after its birth. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, there is no fixed number of days; rather, the validity of denial depends on external indicators that show the husband's acceptance of the child. These indicators include: immediately after birth, at the time when congratulations are offered (and he accepts them or remains silent), or when he purchases supplies for the newborn. If he does any of these, denial becomes invalid. If he has not done any of these, denial is still valid for some time, but if too much time passes without denial, it is no longer valid. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) set a fixed period: the duration of nifas (postpartum bleeding), which is generally 40 days. They argue that a short period (nifas) is acceptable for denial, while a long period is not. They consider nifas as the separator because it is directly tied to childbirth due to legal effects (like prohibition of prayer). The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, if the husband's actions indicate acceptance even within nifas, denial is invalid. Under the Sahibayn, as long as the nifas period has not ended, denial is valid regardless of actions. The authoritative position in the Hanafi school is Imam Abu Hanifa's view, as indicated in the Hidayah, though later fatwa practice sometimes follows the Sahibayn for certainty. The passage also addresses the case of an absent husband: when he returns, the same principles apply based on his knowledge. Authority weight: This is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
نفاس (nifas): Postpartum bleeding period after childbirth, during which a woman is exempt from prayer and fasting.
تهنية (tahni'a): Congratulating someone on a happy occasion, here the birth of a child.
آلة الولادة (alat al-wilada): Supplies or items needed for childbirth, such as clothing or medicines.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on paternity denial?
A) Under Sahibayn, denial is valid only if done immediately; under Abu Hanifa, it can be done at any time.
B) Under Abu Hanifa, denial becomes invalid if the husband shows acceptance (e.g., congratulation); under Sahibayn, it remains valid throughout nifas regardless.
C) Under Sahibayn, denial is always valid; under Abu Hanifa, it is never valid after 40 days.
D) Both positions have the same practical outcome.

Key Takeaway
Paternity denial in Hanafi law is not about a fixed clock but about the father's conduct: once he acts like a father, he cannot later deny the child.

Twins in Li'an: Partial Denial and Its Consequences
Ruling on twin children in li'an: lineage establishment and husband's liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the lineage of twin children when the husband denies one and acknowledges the other, and explain the legal consequences (hadd al-qadhf or li'an) and their qiyas-based proof.

Core Idea
Twins from one pregnancy share one father; partial denial is contradictory, so both are attributed to the husband.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the chapter of li'an: twin children born to a wife, and the husband's inconsistent statement regarding their paternity. Imam al-Quduri presents two variations, each with a different legal outcome for the husband. Understanding this ruling illustrates how Hanafi jurists use qiyas (analogical reasoning) to resolve apparent contradictions in testimony and maintain consistency in lineage (nasab).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two cases: (1) husband denies the first twin and acknowledges the second, (2) husband acknowledges the first and denies the second.
Both scenarios involve twins from a single pregnancy.
2
Understand the common premise
The phrase 'twins created from one water' is the key proof: since both originate from a single act of intercourse, they must share the same father. This is a qiyas based on the physical reality.
This premise overrides any partial denial by the husband.
3
Determine the lineage outcome
In both scenarios, the Hanafi ruling establishes the lineage of both twins with the husband. The denial of one is legally ineffective.
This is the primary ruling: nasab thabit for both.
4
Apply the appropriate consequence for the husband
In scenario 1 (deny first, acknowledge second): the husband's acknowledgment contradicts his denial, proving he lied → he is subject to hadd al-qadhf. In scenario 2 (acknowledge first, deny second): his initial acknowledgment implies he affirmed the wife's chastity; the subsequent denial is a fresh accusation → li'an is required.
The difference hinges on whether his own statement falsifies his denial or creates a new accusation.
5
Connect to the broader principle
The ruling shows that Hanafi law prioritizes consistency in testimony and the physical reality of twinship over partial claims, using qiyas to resolve conflicts.
This illustrates the use of dalil aqli in family law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب اللعان قال واذا ولدت ولدين فى بطن واحد فنفى الاول واعترف بالثانى يثبت نسبهما لانهما توامان خلق من ماء واحد وحد الزوج لانه اكذب نفسه بدعوى الثانى وان اعترف بالاول ونفى الثانى يثبت نسبهما لما ذكرنا ولا عن لانه قاذف بنفى الثانى ولم يرجع عنه والاقرار بالعفة سابق على القذف قصار كما اذاقال انها عفيفة ثم قال هي زانية وفى ذالك التلاعن كذاهذا
Chapter of Li'an. He said: If she gives birth to two children in one womb, and he denies the first and acknowledges the second, both are established as his lineage because they are twins created from one fluid, and the husband is subject to the hadd punishment for slander because he falsified himself by claiming the second. And if he acknowledges the first and denies the second, both are established as his lineage due to what we mentioned, and he must perform li'an because he is slandering by denying the second and does not retract, and the acknowledgment of chastity precedes the slander, similar to if he said she is chaste then said she is an adulteress; in that case li'an is required.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, commented upon in Ashraf al-Hidayah. It addresses a specific case in li'an: when a wife gives birth to twins (defined as children born within less than six months apart from a single pregnancy), and the husband denies the lineage of the first child but acknowledges the second. The ruling establishes both children as his due to the twin nature—they originate from one semen (māʼ wāḥid), so it is impossible that one is his and not the other. However, the husband incurs the hadd punishment for qadhf (false accusation of adultery) because his acknowledgment of the second child contradicts his denial of the first, effectively proving he lied about the first denial. In the second scenario, if he acknowledges the first and denies the second, both children are still established as his lineage for the same reason (twins from one fluid), but here the husband must perform li'an (mutual imprecation) rather than receive hadd. The reason is that his denial of the second child constitutes qadhf against the wife, and he does not retract that accusation. His prior acknowledgment of the first child implies an acknowledgment of the wife's chastity (because acknowledging a child implies marital intercourse and thus the wife's honesty), and that acknowledgment precedes the fresh accusation. This is analogous to a man who first says 'She is chaste' then says 'She is an adulteress'—here li'an is necessary to resolve the contradiction. Thus, the husband's initial implicit confession of chastity takes precedence, preventing hadd but requiring li'an. The commentary then clarifies that the key point is that twins share the same origin, so partial denial is logically inconsistent.

Key Terms
توأمان (taw'amān): Two children born from a single pregnancy (less than six months apart), sharing the same origin from one semen.
لعان (li'ān): Mutual imprecation between spouses when the husband accuses the wife of adultery without witnesses, involving a series of oaths and curses that result in separation.
حد القذف (ḥadd al-qadhf): The fixed punishment (eighty lashes) for falsely accusing a married person of adultery, applicable when the accuser fails to produce four witnesses or retracts the accusation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on twins in li'an, if the husband acknowledges the first twin and denies the second, what is the consequence for the husband?
A) He is subject to hadd al-qadhf.
B) He must perform li'an.
C) Both twins are disowned.
D) He must pay kaffarah.

Key Takeaway
Twins share a common father; partial denial is legally ineffective, and the husband faces hadd or li'an depending on the order of his statements.

Ruling on Impotent Husband: One-Year Respite and Judicial Separation
Ruling on the impotent husband: one-year respite and separation upon wife's demand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the 'unnin, explain the reason for the one-year period, and identify the wife's right to demand judicial separation.

Core Idea
The impotent husband gets one year to prove ability; failure triggers the wife's right to judicial separation.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on marital rights when the husband is impotent ('unnin). The judge grants a year-long respite, after which, if intercourse does not occur, the wife can demand separation. The ruling is based on Companion practice and on distinguishing temporary incapacity from permanent defect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its components
The judge gives a one-year respite; if intercourse occurs within the year, the marriage continues; otherwise, the judge separates the spouses upon the wife's demand.
The separation is a judicial annulment (faskh), not a divorce (talāq).
2
Trace the proof (dalīl)
The ruling is based on the practice of 'Umar, 'Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud (qawl sahābi), which is authoritative in Hanafi uṣūl.
The exact wording 'hākadhā ruwiya 'an 'Umar wa 'Alī wa Ibn Mas'ūd' indicates transmission.
3
Analyze the legal reasoning (ta'līl)
The wife's right to sexual intercourse is established. The husband's failure may be due to a temporary cause or an inherent defect. A period of observation is needed to distinguish the two.
The year covers all four seasons because potency may vary with climate.
4
Determine the operative ruling (muftā bihī)
After the year, if the husband still cannot consummate, he is obligated to release his wife with kindness (tasrīḥ bi-iḥsān). If he refuses, the judge acts on his behalf and annuls the marriage.
The wife's demand is necessary because separation is her right.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العنين وغيره واذا كان الزوج عنينا اجله الحاكم سنة فان وصل اليها فيها والافرق بينهما اذا طلبت المرأة ذالك هكذا روى عن عمر وعلى وابن مسعود ولان الحق ثابت كما فى الوطى ويحتمل ان يكون الامتناع لعلة معترضة ويحتمل لأفة اصلية فلابد من مدة معرفة لذالك وقدرنا ها بالسنة لاشتمالها على الفصول الاربعة فاذا مضت المدة ولم يصل اليها تبين ان العجزبافة اصلية ففات الامساك بالمعروف ووجب عليه التسريح بالاحسان فاذا امنع ناب القاضي منابه ففرق بينهما ولابد من طلبها لان التفريق حقها
Chapter of the Impotent and Others. If the husband is impotent, the judge grants him a year's respite; if he has intercourse with her within that year, well and good; otherwise, the judge separates them if the wife demands it. This is narrated from 'Umar, 'Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud. And because the wife's right is established in sexual intercourse, and it is possible that the refusal is due to a temporary cause, and it is possible that it is due to an inherent defect, so a period of time is necessary to determine that, and we set it as a year because it includes the four seasons. When the period passes and he has not had intercourse with her, it becomes clear that the incapacity is due to an inherent defect; then retaining her with kindness has failed, and he is obligated to release her with kindness. If he refuses, the judge takes his place and separates them. And her demand is necessary because the separation is her right.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the Hanafi ruling on a husband who is 'unnin (impotent). An 'unnin is defined as a man who cannot have vaginal intercourse with women despite having a functioning male organ, whether this arises from a medical condition, natural weakness, old age, or magic. The judge ('al-hākim) gives the husband a one-year respite (ajal) from the time the wife files a complaint. The purpose of the year is to observe the husband's condition across all four seasons, as potency may vary with climate. The ruling is based on reports from the Companions 'Umar, 'Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud, and on the legal reasoning that the wife's right to sexual relations is established (like the right to maintenance), but the husband's failure might be due to a temporary illness (ʿilla ʿāriḍa) requiring treatment or an inherent defect (āfa aṣliyya) that is permanent. The year serves to distinguish the two. If after a year the husband is still unable to have intercourse, it becomes clear that the incapacity is permanent. At that point, the husband has failed to fulfill the Qur'anic command to 'retain them with kindness' (imṣāk bi-maʿrūf), so he must release her with kindness (tasrīḥ bi-iḥsān, i.e., a good divorce). If he refuses to divorce her, the judge steps in as his representative (nāba manābahu) and annuls the marriage (tafrīq baynahumā). The separation is conditional upon the wife's demand, because it is her right. The authorities referenced in the margin (Qadhi Khan, Marghinani, 'Ayni) provide the definition of 'unnin: a man whose organ is limp and motionless, tested by placing him in cold water; if it shrinks, he is not 'unnin; if it stays extended, he is 'unnin. This ruling applies only to vaginal intercourse; a man may be 'unnin with one woman but not another.

Key Terms
عنين ('unnin): A man who is impotent, meaning unable to penetrate and have vaginal intercourse despite having a functioning male organ, due to a defect, weakness, or magic.
اجل (ajal): The one-year respite granted by the judge to the impotent husband to attempt intercourse.
تفريق (tafrīq): Judicial separation (khul' or annulment) ordered by the judge between the spouses when the husband fails to consummate after the respite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling on an impotent husband, why is the respite period set at one year?
A) Because it is the maximum time for medical treatment.
B) Because the Prophet (ﷺ) specifically commanded one year.
C) Because it covers all four seasons to observe changes in potency.
D) Because the wife must wait one year before demanding divorce.

Key Takeaway
The one-year respite for an impotent husband is a Hanafi ruling based on Companion practice, allowing the wife to seek separation if consummation does not occur within a year.

Judicial Separation for Impotence: One-Year Wait and Ba‘in Talaq
Explanation of the ruling on impotent husband and the nature of judicial separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the one-year deadline for an impotent husband and explain why the judicial separation is a ba‘in talaq rather than a faskh, supporting your answer with textual evidence.

Core Idea
The one-year deadline is a judicial investigation; the resulting separation is a ba‘in talaq, not an annulment.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common marital hardship: a wife whose husband is impotent. The Hanafi school prescribes a one-year waiting period to determine if the defect is temporary, then, if it persists, a judicial separation. However, a critical dispute arises: is this separation a divorce (talaq) or an annulment (faskh)? We will walk through the ruling, its proofs, and the inter-madhhab debate with Imam al-Shafi‘i.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and Hanafi procedural ruling
Read the first part of the passage: the wife complains of impotence, the judge gives a one-year deadline from the filing date. If the husband becomes capable within the year, no separation; if not, the judge separates them. Note that this is the mufta bihi position.
The one-year period covers all four seasons to test permanence.
2
Examine the proofs: athar and rational reasoning
The passage cites narrations from Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas‘ud supporting the one-year deadline. Then it provides a rational proof: a year is needed to distinguish temporary from permanent impotence. Identify the authority and logic.
These are sahih athar from the Companions, showing early application.
3
Understand the classification dispute: ba‘in talaq vs. faskh
The text states clearly: the separation is a ba‘in talaq because the judge's act is attributed to the husband. The opposing view of al-Shafi‘i is presented: it should be a faskh. Focus on the Hanafi reasoning: marriage does not accept faskh after completion, and the purpose of removing harm is only achieved by ba‘in.
Imam Malik agrees with the Hanafi view on this point.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to the Shafi‘i proof
Al-Shafi‘i argues that the separation originates from the wife's side, so it cannot be a divorce. The Hanafi rebuttal: the judge's action is attributed to the husband, analogous to a delegated divorce. Also, if it were faskh, the husband could revoke it after separation, prolonging harm.
The Hanafi principle: a completed marriage contract does not accept faskh.
5
Determine the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
List the effects: under Hanafi, separation counts as one ba‘in talaq, full mahr if khalwah occurred, and ‘iddah is wajib. Under Shafi‘i, it is a faskh with no mahr (or half if no khalwah) but ‘iddah by agreement. This shows the importance of the classification.
The Hanafi view is relied upon for fatwa.

Chapter on the Impotent and Others. Because separation is her right. Explanation of the case: If the husband is impotent and his wife demands separation, the judge gives the husband a one-year deadline for treatment, and this one-year begins from the time the woman files a case with the judge. The fatwa is upon this. So if within one year, through treatment or by God's grace, he becomes capable of women and has intercourse with his wife, then praise be to God, no issue. But if after the year passes he remains impotent, then the judge, upon the woman's demand, will separate them. And this is narrated from Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud. Thus it is narrated from Sa'id ibn al-Musayyib: 'Umar ibn al-Khattab ruled that the impotent man is given a one-year respite.' And Imam Muhammad narrated in Kitab al-Athar: 'A woman came to Umar and said her husband does not come to her. He gave him a one-year respite. When the year passed and he did not come to her, he gave her the choice. She chose herself, so Umar separated them and made it a ba'in divorce.' And from Ali: 'Give the impotent man a year; if he comes to her, otherwise separate them.' And from Ibn Mas'ud: 'Give the impotent man a year; if he has intercourse, otherwise separate them.' And the rational proof is that the woman has a right in sexual intercourse, and the husband's abstention could be due to a temporary ailment curable by treatment, or it could be a permanent defect. To investigate this, a period is needed, and we have specified this period as one year because all four seasons occur in a year. So if after a year he still cannot have intercourse, it becomes clear that the inability is due to a permanent defect, and thus the husband has failed to keep her with kindness, so it becomes obligatory for him to release her with kindness. Since he refrained, the judge stands in his place and separates them to remove oppression from the woman. This separation is a ba'in divorce. And that separation is a ba'in divorce because the judge's act is attributed to the husband's act, as if he divorced her himself. Al-Shafi'i said it is a faskh, but according to us, marriage does not accept faskh. It is ba'in because the purpose, which is removing oppression from her, is only achieved by it; if it were not ba'in, she would be hanging by his revocability. She gets her full mahr if he had secluded with her, because the seclusion of an impotent man is valid. And 'iddah is obligatory for the reason mentioned before. All this is when the husband admits that he did not have intercourse with her. Translation: This separation is a ba'in divorce. Because the judge's act is attributed to the husband's act, as if the husband divorced her himself. Imam al-Shafi'i said it is a faskh of marriage, but according to us, marriage does not accept faskh. It is ba'in because the purpose is to remove oppression from the woman, and this is only achieved by a ba'in divorce; if it were not ba'in, the husband could take her back and she would remain in limbo. She gets her full mahr if he had secluded with her, because the seclusion of an impotent man is valid. And 'iddah is obligatory for the reason we mentioned earlier. All this is when the husband admits that he did not reach this woman. Explanation of the issue: The separation that occurs between the impotent man and his wife through the judge is a ba'in divorce; Imam Malik holds this view. Imam al-Shafi'i says this separation is a faskh of the marriage. Imam al-Shafi'i's proof is that this separation comes from the woman's side, and what comes from the woman's side can be her separation but cannot be divorce because the woman does not have the right to divorce. Our proof is that the judge's act is attributed to the husband, as if the husband divorced her himself. As for Imam al-Shafi'i's saying that this separation is a faskh, we reply: marriage does not accept faskh after completion, but before completion it does accept faskh, as in the option of puberty and the option of emancipation. As for why it is ba'in: the purpose of the judge's separation is to remove oppression from the woman, and this purpose can only be achieved by a ba'in divorce. If this separation were not ba'in, by the husband taking her back, this woman would remain hanging, because the purpose of removing harm would not be achieved. And if the impotent man has secluded with his wife, then his wife is entitled to the full mahr. The proof is that the seclusion of an impotent man is valid. So when the woman submitted her private part with the existence of the tool, the husband becomes liable for its substitute (mahr). If he did not seclude, half the mahr is due. And since according to Imam al-Shafi'i this separation is a faskh of marriage, for this woman there is no mahr and no maintenance; however, 'iddah is obligatory by agreement. The previous proof that there is a possibility of the womb being occupied, so out of precaution, 'iddah was made obligatory by istihsan. The author of al-Hidayah says: that the impotent man is given a one-year deadline, and after one year...
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the judicial separation (tafriq) due to impotence. It begins by stating the Hanafi ruling: if a wife complains of her husband's impotence, the judge grants a one-year period from the date the case is filed. If after a year the husband remains unable to have intercourse, the judge separates them. This ruling is supported by narrations from Caliphs Umar, Ali, and Ibn Mas'ud, and it is the position upon which fatwa is given. The rational proof for the one-year period is that it covers all four seasons, allowing time to determine whether the defect is temporary or permanent. If the husband fails to consummate after a year, it is clear that he cannot fulfill the wife's right, so the judge acts in his stead to remove harm. The passage then addresses the nature of this separation. The Hanafi (and Maliki) position is that it is a ba'in talaq (one irrevocable divorce), because the judge's act is attributed to the husband. Imam al-Shafi'i argues it is a faskh (annulment) since the separation originates from the wife's side. The Hanafi response explains that marriage does not accept faskh after completion; rather, the judge's action is analogized to the husband's own divorce. The practical consequence of this dispute is significant: in the Hanafi school, if khalwah (seclusion) occurred, the wife receives her full mahr and must observe 'iddah; the separation counts as one ba'in talaq, which prohibits remarriage without a new contract and a new mahr. In contrast, under the Shafi'i view, it would be a complete annulment with no mahr (or half if no khalwah) and no 'iddah (though the text states that 'iddah is wajib by agreement). The passage concludes that if the husband admits he did not have intercourse, the above rulings apply.

Key Terms
العنين (al-'innin): An impotent man who cannot perform sexual intercourse due to a permanent or temporary defect.
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot revoke it during the 'iddah, and remarriage requires a new contract.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract ab initio (as if it never existed) without a divorce count.
خلوت (khalwah): Seclusion between husband and wife that is valid and lawful, even if no intercourse occurs.
عدت ('iddah): The waiting period after divorce or separation during which the woman cannot remarry.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the legal nature of the separation when an impotent husband fails to consummate after the one-year period?
A) It is a faskh (annulment) of the marriage.
B) It is a ba'in talaq (irrevocable divorce).
C) It is a revocable divorce (raj'i).
D) It is a conditional divorce that becomes effective only if the wife pays compensation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats judicial separation for impotence as a ba'in talaq after a one-year waiting period, distinguishing it from annulment through the principle of attributing the judge's action to the husband.

Separation Due to Impotence: Ba'in Divorce vs Faskh – Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute
Ruling that judge's separation is ba'in talaq with proof and Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that judicial separation due to impotence is a ba'in divorce, explain why it is not an annulment (faskh), and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The judge's separation is a ba'in divorce because marriage does not accept annulment; the harmful suspense would remain if it were revocable.

This passage addresses a critical dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the legal nature of judicial separation for an impotent husband (al-‘innin). The Hanafi school holds that such separation is an irrevocable divorce (talaq ba'in) because the judge's action is legally attributed to the husband, making it as if he divorced her himself. Imam al-Shafi'i considers it an annulment (faskh) since the separation originates from the wife's request. Understanding this difference is essential for determining the number of remaining divorces and the mahr obligations.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that the Hanafi school rules the separation as a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce. The judge's action is attributed to the husband, so it is as if he divorced her.
Hanafi: talaq ba'in sughra – husband cannot take wife back without a new marriage contract.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the separation is an annulment (faskh), not a divorce. This is because the separation originates from the wife's request, and the wife cannot give divorce.
Shafi'i: faskh cancels the marriage contract ab initio for some purposes.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i proof: since the wife petitions the judge, the ruling is based on her right, not the husband's action. A divorce must be initiated by the husband, so it cannot be talaq.
Shafi'i usul: divorce is the husband's prerogative; annulment can be granted by the judge based on defect.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: a completed marriage contract cannot be annulled; annulment (faskh) is only possible before the contract is complete (e.g., options of option). The judge's action is attributed to the husband as if he divorced her, making it a talaq.
Hanafi principle: 'The judge's act is attributed to the husband because the husband is the one obligated to consummate.' Also, the marriage does not accept faskh after consummation or valid seclusion.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, the separation counts as one ba'in divorce, reducing the husband's available divorces. Under Shafi'i, it is a faskh and does not affect the divorce count. Also, mahr and 'iddah rules differ: Hanafi gives full mahr if there was valid seclusion (khalwa), Shafi'i may not.
Practical consequence: in Hanafi, if the husband later wishes to remarry, he needs a new mahr and contract; in Shafi'i, the couple can reconcile within 'iddah as if annulled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Al-Shafi'i said it is an annulment, but according to us, marriage does not accept annulment. It occurs as ba'in because the objective, which is removing harm from her, is only achieved by that. If it were not ba'in, she would return suspended upon reconciliation. And she is entitled to her full dowry if he had seclusion with her, for the seclusion of the impotent man is valid; and the waiting period as we previously stated. This is when the husband admits he did not have intercourse with her.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage presents the ruling that when a judge separates an impotent husband from his wife, the separation is a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce according to the Hanafi school. The reasoning is that the judge's act is legally attributed to the husband's action, as if he divorced her himself. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that it is an annulment (faskh), not a divorce. The Hanafi response is that marriage does not accept annulment in such cases; annulment is only possible before the contract is complete (e.g., options of puberty or emancipation). The separation must be ba'in because the purpose is to remove harm from the wife, which would not be achieved if it were a revocable divorce (since the husband could take her back, leaving her in a suspended state). The wife is entitled to the full mahr if the husband had valid seclusion (khalwa) with her, because the seclusion of an impotent man is considered valid for the purpose of establishing mahr and 'iddah. The ruling applies specifically when the husband admits that he did not have intercourse with her.

Key Terms
طلاق بائن (talaq ba'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take back the wife without a new marriage and her consent.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of marriage contract, treating it as if it never existed for some purposes.
خلوت (khalwa): Valid seclusion between husband and wife that is legally considered as consummation for mahr and 'iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) A voidable marriage (fasid)

Key Takeaway
Judicial separation for impotence is a ba'in divorce under Hanafi law because marriage does not accept annulment and the harm removal demands irrevocability.

Mahr and 'Iddah in Impotence Separation
Ruling on full mahr and 'iddah when khalwa occurred before separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on mahr and 'iddah when valid seclusion occurred before separation due to impotence, and explain the legal effect of khalwa sahiha.

Core Idea
Valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) triggers full mahr and 'iddah even without intercourse — availability suffices.

In cases of separation due to impotence, the question arises whether the wife is entitled to full mahr and must observe 'iddah. The Hanafi school holds that if valid seclusion occurred before the separation, the wife receives the full mahr and must wait, because she made herself available. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The passage rules on the wife's entitlement to full mahr and obligation of 'iddah when impotence leads to separation after valid seclusion.
2
Recognise the key condition
The decisive factor is 'khalwa sahiha' (valid seclusion). If it occurred before separation, the full mahr and 'iddah apply.
Without valid seclusion, only half mahr is due.
3
Understand the basis
The seclusion of an impotent man is legally valid (sahiha) because the wife submitted herself; thus it is equivalent to actual intercourse for mahr and 'iddah.
This is a Hanafi specific rule.
4
Note the implication
Even though no intercourse occurred, the full mahr is payable and the 'iddah is required due to the potential of pregnancy or precaution (istihsan).
5
Contrast with other schools
The Shafi'i school requires actual intercourse for full mahr, highlighting the distinctive Hanafi position.
See the madhhab dispute map for details.

Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage extracts the rule regarding mahr and 'iddah in the context of separation due to impotence. If the husband had valid seclusion (khalwa sahiha) with his wife before the separation, she is entitled to the full mahr. The reason is that the seclusion of an impotent man is legally valid (sahiha) for the purpose of establishing mahr and 'iddah. Even though actual intercourse did not occur, the wife submitted herself and made herself available, so the full mahr is payable. Additionally, the wife must observe 'iddah (waiting period) after the separation, as previously established in the text, because the possibility of pregnancy exists if seclusion occurred, or out of precaution (istihsan) to avoid uncertainty about the womb.

Key Terms

Dispute Between Spouses on Consummation: Thayyib vs. Bikr
Rules for credibility in consummation dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving a dispute over consummation between spouses, distinguishing between a thayyib and a bikr wife.

Core Idea
The presumption of soundness (asalat al-salamah) makes the husband's oath decisive for a thayyib, while for a bikr, women's inspection determines the procedure.

In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband claims consummation and the wife denies, the ruling depends on the wife's status at marriage. The legal presumption that the husband's organ is sound shifts the burden of proof. This passage from al-Hidayah explains the procedure with practical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute and wife's status
Determine whether the wife is a thayyib (previously married) or a bikr (virgin at marriage), as the procedure differs.
Thayyib status is based on prior intercourse, not just marriage.
2
Apply the rule for thayyib
If thayyib, the husband's word with an oath is accepted because he denies the wife's right to separation, and the original state (asalat al-salamah) presumes his organ sound.
The husband is the defendant (mudda‘a 'alaih).
3
Understand the proof: asalat al-salamah
The legal principle that the original state of the human body is soundness justifies the husband's denial being accepted with his oath.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli).
4
Apply the rule for bikr
If bikr, women (female experts) inspect her. If they confirm virginity, the husband's claim is false, so he gets a year respite. If they say she is thayyib, the husband's oath is taken.
Inspection by women is a form of evidence.
5
Determine consequences of oath and nukul
If the husband swears, the wife loses her right to seek separation. If he refuses (nukul), he is given one year to attempt consummation.
Nukul leads to respite, not immediate judgment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلف الزوج و المرأة فى الوصول اليهافان كانت ثيبا فالقول قوله مع يمينه لانه ينكر استحقاق حق الفرقة والاصل هو السلامة فى الجبلة ثم ان حلف بطل حقهاوان نكل يؤجل سنة وانكانت بكر انظر اليها النساء فان قلن هى بكراجل سنة لظهور كذبه وان قلن هى ثيب يحلف الزوج فان حلف لاحق لها وان نكل يؤجل سنة
And if the husband and wife differ regarding having reached her: if she is a thayyib (previously married), then the word is his word with his oath, because he denies the entitlement to the right of separation, and the original state is the soundness of the organ. Then if he swears, her right is nullified; if he refuses, he is given a year's respite. And if she is a bikr (virgin), the women inspect her. If they say she is a virgin, he is given a year because his lie is apparent. If they say she is a thayyib, the husband takes an oath; if he swears, there is no right for her; if he refuses, he is given a year.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage addresses the scenario where the husband claims he has consummated the marriage, but the wife denies it. The ruling depends on whether the wife is a thayyib (previously married and thus not a virgin at marriage) or a bikr (virgin at marriage). If she is a thayyib, the husband's statement with an oath is accepted because he is denying the wife's right to seek separation based on impotence; the legal presumption is that his sexual organ is sound (original state of health). Thus, the husband is considered the defendant (mudda‘a 'alaih), and his word with oath prevails. If he swears, the wife loses her right. If he refuses to swear (nukul), then he is given a year to attempt treatment/cohabitation. If the wife is a bikr, then women (female experts) inspect her. If they confirm she is still a virgin, the husband is given a year because his claim of consummation is apparently false. If they say she is no longer a virgin (i.e., thayyib), then the husband's oath is taken; if he swears he did consummate, the wife has no right; if he refuses, he gets a year.

Key Terms
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has had prior sexual intercourse (whether through marriage or otherwise).
بكر (bikr): A virgin woman who has never had intercourse.
نكول (nukul): Refusal to take an oath; which, in this case, leads to granting the husband a one-year respite.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In a consummation dispute, the Hanafi ruling hinges on the wife's status: for a thayyib, the husband's oath prevails due to asalat al-salamah; for a bikr, women's inspection determines the further procedure.

Dispute Over Consummation: Thayyib vs Bikr
Ruling on whose statement is accepted when husband and wife differ over consummation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whose statement is accepted when a husband and wife dispute consummation, and apply the distinction between thayyib and bikr.

Core Idea
The husband's word with oath is accepted for a thayyib wife; for a bikr, physical inspection decides.

This lesson examines a procedural ruling from the Hanafi school on a family dispute: the husband claims consummation, the wife denies it. The ruling depends on whether the wife is thayyib (previously married) or bikr (virgin). The underlying rationale is the presumption of physical soundness and the evidentiary value of virginity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
The husband and wife disagree on whether consummation occurred. This determines the wife's right to seek separation.
The dispute is considered before the qadi; the marriage is valid.
2
Determine the wife's status
The first factor is whether she is thayyib or bikr. This can be established by her own claim or by physical inspection.
The Hanafi school applies different evidentiary rules based on this status.
3
Apply the ruling for thayyib
If she is thayyib, the husband's word with oath is accepted because he denies the entitlement to separation and the original presumption is soundness of his genitals.
If he swears, her right is void; if he refuses, he gets one year.
4
Apply the ruling for bikr
If she is bikr, women inspect her. If they say she is a virgin, the husband is false and given one year immediately. If they say she is thayyib, the husband takes an oath.
Oath: if sworn, no right; if refused, one year.
5
Understand the ta'lil
The reasoning is based on al-asl al-salamah (original state is soundness) and the use of physical evidence to resolve the dispute.
The ruling balances presumption of innocence with tangible proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اختلف الزوج و المرأة فى الوصول اليها فان كانت ثيبا فالقول قوله مع يمينه لانه ينكر استحقاق حق الفرقة والاصل هو السلامة فى الجبلة ثم ان حلف بطل حقها وان نكل يؤجل سنة وان كانت بكرا انظر اليها النساء فان قلن هى بكر اجل سنة لظهور كذبه وان قلن هى ثيب يحلف الزوج فان حلف لاحق لها وان نكل يؤجل سنة

Then if he swears, her right is invalidated; and if he refuses, he is deferred one year. And if she is a virgin, women inspect her; and if they say she is a virgin, he is deferred one year because of the appearance of his falsehood; and if they say she is thayyib, the husband takes an oath; if he swears, there is no right for her; and if he refuses, he is deferred one year.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling (hukm) from the Hanafi school regarding a dispute between husband and wife about whether consummation occurred. The legal issue is one of judicial proof (bayyinah) and the burden of proof. The ruling distinguishes between two states of the wife: thayyib (non-virgin, previously married) and bikr (virgin). For a thayyib wife, the husband's statement is accepted with his oath. The reason (ta'lil) given is that he is denying the wife's claim that she is entitled to the right of separation (firqah) due to his impotence or failure to consummate; the original presumption (asl) is that his generative organ is sound and functional. Thus, the husband is effectively the defendant (mudda'a alayh) and his word with oath is accepted. If he swears, the wife's right to demand separation is invalidated. If he refuses the oath (nukul), he is given a one-year respite (ajal) to allow for treatment or natural recovery. For a virgin wife (bikr), the matter is different: since her hymen is intact, it is evidence that consummation did not occur. Therefore, the qadi appoints women to inspect her. If they declare her a virgin, the husband's claim of consummation is false, and he is immediately given a one-year respite (without the option of oath). If the women say she is thayyib (hymen broken), the husband's claim becomes plausible, so he is given an oath; if he swears, the wife has no right; if he refuses, he is also given a year's respite. The commentary from 'Inayah adds practical methods for virginity testing, but these are not part of the core ruling.

Key Terms
ثيب (thayyib): A woman who has been previously married and has lost her virginity through intercourse.
بكر (bikr): A virgin woman who has not had intercourse.
يمين (yamin): An oath taken by the husband before the qadi to support his claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Ruling on Majbub and Khasi Husbands
Ruling on immediate separation for the excised husband and the castrated husband's analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for immediate separation (majbub) vs. one-year respite (khasi) and explain the qiyas that justifies the difference.

Core Idea
Immediate separation for the amputated man; one-year wait for the castrated man because his intercourse is still hoped for.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses two distinct cases of male sexual dysfunction and their implications for the wife's right to seek separation. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the possibility of recovery: the majbub (penis amputated) has no hope, so separation is immediate; the khasi (castrated but with organ intact) retains hope, so he is given a one-year respite like the aneen. Understanding this distinction requires following the qiyas (analogy) that links the khasi to the impotent man.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Note the two terms: majbub (penis amputated) and khasi (testicles removed, penis intact). Their conditions determine the ruling.
The majbub is irreversible; the khasi has potential for recovery.
2
State the ruling for majbub
The text says: 'separation is made between them immediately if she demands it'. The wife may seek immediate separation from the qadi without any waiting period.
Reason: no benefit in respite (la fa'idah fi al-ta'jil).
3
State the ruling for khasi
The khasi is given a respite (yujjalu) like the impotent man (aneen). This means a one-year waiting period before separation can be granted.
The text does not specify one year, but the standard Hanafi position is one year.
4
Analyze the qiyas
The khasi is analogized to the aneen because the illa (effective cause) is the possibility of future intercourse (wat'uhu marju). Since the penis remains, there is hope; thus he gets the same respite.
This is a qiyas jaliy – the common illa is clear and explicit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان كان مجبو با فرق بينهما في الحال ان طلبت لانه لافائدة فى التاجيل والخصى يوجل كما يؤجل العنين لان وطيه مرجو
And if he is excised (majbub), separation is made between them immediately if she demands it, because there is no benefit in the respite. And the castrated man (khasi) is given respite as the impotent man (aneen) is given respite, because his intercourse is hoped for.
Source pages p. 161

Commentary
This passage addresses two types of male sexual dysfunctions: (1) majbub (مجبوب) – a man whose penis has been completely amputated, and (2) khasi (خصى) – a man whose testicles have been removed (castrated) but whose penis remains intact. For the majbub, the Hanafi ruling is that the wife can obtain an immediate separation (tafriq) from the qadi if she demands it, without any waiting period (ajal). The reason given is that there is no benefit (fa'idah) in deferring because his condition is irreversible; there is no hope of cure or restoration of sexual function. For the khasi, however, the ruling is that he is given a one-year respite (yujjalu) just like the impotent man (aneen). The basis for this analogy (qiyas) is that his intercourse is still hoped for (wat'uhu marju) because the penis remains, and with time, he may regain sexual ability. Thus, the khasi is treated identically to an aneen in terms of the waiting period before the wife may seek separation. The commentary emphasizes the distinction based on the potential for recovery.

Key Terms
مجبوب (majbub): A man whose penis has been amputated.
خصى (khasi): A castrated man whose testicles have been removed but whose penis remains.
تأجيل (ta'jil): The act of granting a deferment or respite period for the husband to seek treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants immediate separation for the majbub but gives a one-year respite for the khasi, based on the possibility of recovery.
